    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="cc67f03a8ab84508b75e129803c66477">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'ac406c39-e381-47d5-924a-6f5060e2b1a7'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d208945189" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>P2-type Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is a promising cathode for sodium-ion batteries with features of high specific capacity and air resistance, whereas its cycling stability and rate performance are dissatisfactory&nbsp;suffering from the disastrous P2-O2 phase transition and Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering during sodium-ion de/intercalation, which makes it an obstruction for future practical applications. Herein, a delicate multicomponent modulation strategy is proposed to tackle these two issues simultaneously, in which Li<sup>+</sup> and Ti<sup>4+</sup> are introduced to replace the Ni<sup>2+</sup> and Mn<sup>4+</sup>, respectively, whereas the Na<sup>+</sup> content is also designed according to the principle of charge balance. Consequently, the designed cathode (Na<sub>0.72</sub>Ni<sub>0.28</sub>Li<sub>0.05</sub>Mn<sub>0.57</sub>Ti<sub>0.10</sub>O<sub>2</sub>) can deliver an enchanting cycling stability of 80% at 1 C after 200 cycles along with a considerable rate performance of 82.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 C. In situ X-ray diffraction measurement demonstrates the destructive P2-O2 phase transition is suppressed and converted into a P2-Z phase transition with superior reversibility as well as smooth charge/discharge curves with better Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy disordering. In addition, the full cell matched with hard carbon anode delivers an excellent energy density of 263.4 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at 37.3 W kg<sup>−1</sup>, exhibiting great practicality. Our work presents a mean&nbsp;to rationally design the component of layered oxide cathode and achieve fabulous performance for sodium ion batteries.</p></div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="bte220230022-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="bte220230022-sec-0010-title">1 INTRODUCTION</h2>
         
         <p>Renewable energy resources such as solar and wind energy have attracted increasing attention from researchers, expecting to replace the traditional fossil fuels.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0001_R_d208945176e423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nature">1</a>-<a href="#bte220230022-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0003_R_d208945176e423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat Commun">3</a></sup></span> However, it is unfortunate&nbsp;that such types of energy cannot be supplied to the grid directly due to the drawbacks of regionality and discontinuity, which makes it necessary for it to be stored by energy storage systems (EESs) first.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0004_R_d208945176e426" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat Energy">4</a>-<a href="#bte220230022-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0006_R_d208945176e426" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Angew Chem Int Ed">6</a></sup></span> Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been extensively used in diverse scenarios by virtue of gorgeous energy/power density and prominent lifespan.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0005_R_d208945176e429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv Energy Mater">5</a>, <a href="#bte220230022-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0007_R_d208945176e429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - ACS Cent Sci">7</a>, <a href="#bte220230022-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0008_R_d208945176e429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nat Sustain">8</a></sup></span> However, the limitation of lithium resources and soaring cost hinder the application of LIBs, compelling the development of cost-effective alternative techniques.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0009_R_d208945176e432" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv Funct Mater">9</a></sup></span> Up to now, many categories of alternatives have been studied, including sodium ion batteries, potassium ion batteries, zinc ion batteries and so on.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0010_R_d208945176e435" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Batteries Supercaps">10</a>-<a href="#bte220230022-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0012_R_d208945176e435" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - eScience">12</a></sup></span> Recently, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have received significant attention by similar working mechanisms with LIBs as well as the abundance of sodium resources. However, developing suitable cathode materials for SIBs is still a challenge due to the unsatisfactory capacity and stability caused by the larger radius (1.02 Å vs. 0.76 Å) and heavier mass (23 g mol<sup>−1</sup> vs. 6.9 g mol<sup>−1</sup>) compared with lithium ions, which immensely restricts its practical application.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0013_R_d208945176e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ Sci">13</a>-<a href="#bte220230022-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0016_R_d208945176e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - eScience">16</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Up to now, various kinds of materials have been intensively researched as possible cathodes for SIBs, including layered transition metal oxides (LTMOs), polyanionic compounds, Prussian blue analogs (PBAs) and organic cathodes.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0017_R_d208945176e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - eScience">17</a>, <a href="#bte220230022-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0018_R_d208945176e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - eScience">18</a></sup></span> Among the multifarious cathodes, sodium-based LTMOs (Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub>, 0 &lt; <i>x</i> ≤ 1, TM = Mn, Ni, Fe, Co, Cr, and so on) are expected to be the most hopeful candidates on account of the characterizations of smooth synthesis process, high specific capacity and compact crystal structure.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0019_R_d208945176e458" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Angew Chem Int Ed">19</a>-<a href="#bte220230022-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0021_R_d208945176e458" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Angew Chem">21</a></sup></span> Generally speaking, Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> can be classified into P2 (Na<sup>+</sup> occupy the prismatic sites while oxygen atoms stacking sequences are ABBA) and O3 (Na<sup>+</sup> occupy the octahedral sites, whereas oxygen atoms stacking sequences are ABCABC) structures according to the different coordination environment of sodium ions and the stacking sequences of oxygen atoms.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0022_R_d208945176e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Environ Sci">22</a>, <a href="#bte220230022-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0023_R_d208945176e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv Energy Mater">23</a></sup></span> Noteworthily, P2-type materials possess spacious and direct sodium diffusion paths, as well as better air stability, exhibiting better rate and cycling performance. Particularly, Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NNMO) as a representative material of the P2-type structure&nbsp;possesses a high theoretical specific capacity of 173 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> along with a high operating voltage of 3.7 V when cycled within a specific voltage range.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0024_R_d208945176e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J Electrochem Soc">24</a>-<a href="#bte220230022-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0027_R_d208945176e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - eScience">27</a></sup></span> Unfortunately, it undergoes a P2-O2 phase transition with large volume change (about 23%) when charged above 4.2 V, resulting in the destruction of crystal structure and degradation of electrochemical performance.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0025_R_d208945176e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv Energy Mater">25</a>, <a href="#bte220230022-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0028_R_d208945176e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Energy Storage Mater">28</a>-<a href="#bte220230022-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0030_R_d208945176e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv Sci">30</a></sup></span> Moreover, the complicated Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering rearrangement, which is evidenced by apparent voltage plateaus in the charge/discharge curves severely affects the sodium storage capacity and cycling stability.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0031_R_d208945176e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nano Energy">31</a>-<a href="#bte220230022-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0033_R_d208945176e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Sci Adv">33</a></sup></span> Therefore, it is of great significance to develop the cathode material with suppressed phase transition and Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering, thus achieving better electrochemical performance and practicality.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we propose&nbsp;a high voltage stable material Na<sub>0.72</sub>Ni<sub>0.28</sub>Li<sub>0.05</sub>Mn<sub>0.57</sub>Ti<sub>0.10</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (denoted as NNLMTO) with delicate multicomponent modulation to address the phase transition and Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering problems simultaneously. Specifically, the Li<sup>+</sup> and Ti<sup>4+</sup> are introduced to replace the Ni<sup>2+</sup> and Mn<sup>4+</sup>, respectively, whereas the Na<sup>+</sup> content is also designed for the principle of charge balance. Although single Li<sup>+</sup> or Ti<sup>4+</sup> doping has been reported, but the Li<sup>+</sup>, Ti<sup>4+</sup>, and Na<sup>+</sup> comodulation have not been reported. According to previous reports, Li<sup>+</sup> substitution can help to build a high sodium content P2 phase, thus increasing the theoretical capacity, and due to the unequal charge between Li<sup>+</sup> and Ni<sup>2+</sup>, the Na<sup>+</sup> content is increased, which will reduce the electrostatic repulsion between adjacent O layer and stabilize the Na layer. However, Li<sup>+</sup> doping will cause the reduction of operating voltage, whereas Ti<sup>4+</sup> substitution is able to enhance the ionicity of the crystal lattice and raise the redox potential, thus Ti<sup>4+</sup> is introduced to complement the operating voltage.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0034_R_d208945176e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Science">34</a>-<a href="#bte220230022-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0040_R_d208945176e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J Energy Chem">40</a></sup></span> In addition, both Li<sup>+</sup> and Ti<sup>4+</sup> substitution can promote Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy disordering, resulting in a smoother and faster de/intercalation process of sodium ions.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0033_R_d208945176e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Sci Adv">33</a>, <a href="#bte220230022-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0041_R_d208945176e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Chem Mater">41</a>-<a href="#bte220230022-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0043_R_d208945176e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Adv Mater">43</a></sup></span> Consequently, the NNLMTO cathode delivers an excellent capacity of 128 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a high operating voltage of 3.64 V at 0.1 C (1 C = 173 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), as well as an enchanting cycling stability of 80%&nbsp;after 200 cycles at 1 C. Besides, it can also deliver a considerable rate performance of 82.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 C and recover 96% when the current density is reduced to 0.1 C. In situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) demonstrates that the destructive P2-O2 phase transition is suppressed and converts into a P2-Z phase transition with superior reversibility, which eminently improves the stability of the crystal structure. Smooth charge/discharge curves along with density functional theory (DFT) calculations reveal that better Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy disordering is realized thanks to the delicate multicomponent modulation strategy. Commendably, beyond the gorgeous performance in half cell, the coin-type full cell based on NNLMTO cathode and hard carbon (HC) anode delivers an excellent energy density of 263.4 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at 37.3 W kg<sup>−1</sup>, and a pouch-type full cell can provide with a fabulous capacity retention of 81.2% after 100 cycles at 0.1 C, exhibiting great potential in practical application for SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="bte220230022-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="bte220230022-sec-0020-title">2 RESULTS AND DISCUSSION</h2>
         
         <p>The NNLMTO and control samples of NNMO, Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.52</sub>Ti<sub>0.15</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (denoted as NNMTO), and Na<sub>0.82</sub>Ni<sub>0.18</sub>Li<sub>0.15</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (denoted as NNLMO) are synthesized by a typical sol-gel method (see Section&nbsp;<a class="sectionLink scrollableLink" href="#bte220230022-sec-0040">4</a> for detail). The XRD and corresponding Rietveld refinement (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0001">1A,B</a> and Supporting Information: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>) are carried out to investigate the detailed crystallographic structure of the samples. All the main peaks can be assigned to the hexagonal P2-type structure with the space group of P6<sub>3</sub>/mmc for the four samples, excepting a trace amount of NiO impurities in the NNMO and NNMTO, which can be attributed to the limited solubility of Ni<sup>2+</sup> in a layered structure,<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0044_R_d208945176e623" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Solid State Ionics">44</a></sup></span> indicating the good crystallinity for all samples. Noteworthily, two superstructure peaks located at 27.2° and 28.3° are mainly originated from the in-plane Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering, which is observed in the NNMO sample.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0045_R_d208945176e629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - J Electrochem Soc">45</a>, <a href="#bte220230022-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0046_R_d208945176e629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Chem Mater">46</a></sup></span> However, these superlattice peaks disappear in the other three samples (NNLMTO, NNLMO, and NNMTO), indicating that the Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering can be effectively inhibited through Li<sup>+</sup> and Ti<sup>4+</sup> substitution. The XRD refinement results (Supporting Information: Tables&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>) show that Li<sup>+</sup> and Ti<sup>4+</sup> are successfully incorporated into the layered structure. In addition, after the multicomponent modulation, the lattice parameter of <i>an</i> increased due to the larger ionic radius of Li<sup>+</sup> and Ti<sup>4+</sup> compared with Ni<sup>2+</sup> and Mn<sup>4+</sup>, whereas the lattice parameter of <i>c</i> decreased, which may be attributed to more Na<sup>+</sup> content in the sodium layer, which is helpful for the stability under deep desodiation state. Besides, the Na<sub><i>e</i></sub>/Na<sub><i>f</i></sub> ratio increases from 1.71 to 1.83 in NNLMTO after multicomponent modulation, indicating the Na<sup>+</sup> rearrangement occurs in the sodium layer and realizes better Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy disordering.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0047_R_d208945176e675" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Nano Energy">47</a></sup></span> The detailed P2-type structure can be schematically described in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0001">1C</a>, in which the transition metal-oxygen (MO<sub>6</sub>) octahedrons share edges with each other to form the transition metal layer (TMO<sub>2</sub>), whereas Na<sup>+</sup> occupies&nbsp;the prismatic sites between two adjacent TMO<sub>2</sub>. The morphologies of the synthesized samples are surveyed by field-emission scanning electron microcopy, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0001">1D</a> and Supporting Information: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, in which all the samples show typically plate-like morphology of P2-type structure as previously reported while the morphology of NNLMTO, NNLMO, and NNMTO samples become smoother due to the Li<sup>+</sup> and Ti<sup>4+</sup> substitution.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0048_R_d208945176e700" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Nat Commun">48</a></sup></span> The transmission electron microscope (TEM) image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0001">1E</a>) further validates the plate-like feature of NNLMTO. The high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) is used to survey the microstructure of NNLMTO. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0001">1F</a>, the HRTEM image exhibits a clear lattice fringe with an&nbsp;interlayer spacing of 2.27 Å that can be indexed to the (012) plane of the P2-type structure, which confirms the well-crystalline P2-type structure. The elemental mapping results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0001">1G</a>) illustrate the elements of Na, Ni, Mn, Ti, and O distributed uniformly throughout the whole particle of NNLMTO. In brief, the structural and morphological analyses illustrate that NNLMTO and control samples are successfully synthesized and better Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy disordering is achieved through such a multicomponent modulation strategy.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="bte220230022-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/55e8087f-5620-4ec0-8e74-d0503b9ae79a/bte220230022-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/55e8087f-5620-4ec0-8e74-d0503b9ae79a/bte220230022-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4c3cc070-de97-402a-93ae-200e8b7a098a/bte220230022-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/55e8087f-5620-4ec0-8e74-d0503b9ae79a/bte220230022-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=bte220230022-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbte2.20230022" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural and morphological analyses: Rietveld refinement of the X-ray diffraction patterns for P2-Na<sub>0.72</sub>Ni<sub>0.28</sub>Li<sub>0.05</sub>Mn<sub>0.57</sub>Ti<sub>0.10</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NNLMTO) (A) and P2-Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NNMO) (B). (C) The schematic crystal structure diagram of P2-NNLMTO. SEM image (D) and transmission electron microscope (TEM) image (E) of NNLMTO. (F) High-resolution TEM analysis of NNLMTO. (G) Elemental mapping results of P2-NNLMTO.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) is executed for the sake of investigating the chemical state of NNLMTO samples. The survey spectrum (Supporting Information: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3A</a>) shows that the elements of Na, Ni, Mn, Ti, and O exist, whereas the Li signal is hard to detect in the survey spectrum and Li 1<i>s</i> high-resolution spectrum (Supporting Information: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3E</a>), which is ascribed to the small photoionization cross section and low content of Li.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0049_R_d208945176e768" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Electrochim Acta">49</a></sup></span> The Mn 2<i>p</i> high-resolution spectrum is shown in Supporting Information: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3B</a>, in which two peaks located at 653.9 and 642.4 eV attributed to the Mn 2<i>p</i><sub>1/2</sub> and Mn2<i>p</i><sub>3/2</sub>, respectively, can be observed, indicating that the chemical state of Mn is tetravalent.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0050_R_d208945176e783" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Chem Eng J">50</a>, <a href="#bte220230022-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0051_R_d208945176e783" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Chem Mater">51</a></sup></span> Ni 2<i>p</i> high-resolution spectrum (Supporting Information: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3C</a>) shows four peaks, in which the characteristic peaks at the binding energy of 872.2 and 854.7 eV can be attributed to the Ni 2<i>p</i><sub>1/2</sub> and Ni 2<i>p</i><sub>3/2</sub>, and their corresponding satellite peaks are located at 878.8 and 861.1 eV, respectively, indicating the chemical state of Ni is divalent.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0050_R_d208945176e798" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Chem Eng J">50</a>, <a href="#bte220230022-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0052_R_d208945176e798" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Joule">52</a></sup></span> Supporting Information: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3D</a> shows the Ti 2<i>p</i> high-resolution spectrum and two peaks at 463.7 and 457.9 eV corresponding to the Ti 2<i>p</i><sub>1/2</sub> and Ti 2<i>p</i><sub>3/2</sub>, respectively, can be identified, indicating that Ti is tetravalent.</p>
         
         <p>The comparison&nbsp;investigation of electrochemical performance&nbsp;on NNLMTO and NNMO is performed to demonstrate the effects caused by multicomponent modulation. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0002">2A</a> shows the first three cycle charge/discharge curves of P2-NNMO at 0.1 C (1 C = 173 mA g<sup>−1</sup>), which delivers a specific capacity of 149.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> along with three obvious plateaus. The apparent plateaus observed in charge/discharge curves can also be indicated in cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0002">2C</a>), in which the redox peaks below 4 V are attributed to the sequential oxidation of Ni<sup>2+</sup>-Ni<sup>3+</sup>-Ni<sup>4+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering in the sodium layer, and the anodic peak above 4 V is relative to the P2-O2 phase transition.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0024_R_d208945176e835" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J Electrochem Soc">24</a>, <a href="#bte220230022-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0026_R_d208945176e835" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Angew Chem">26</a>, <a href="#bte220230022-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0053_R_d208945176e835" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - J Mater Chem A">53</a></sup></span> However, the charge/discharge curves of NNLMTO (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0002">2B</a>) become smoother and the plateaus under 4 V almost disappear along with the shortened plateau above 4 V, which is also confirmed by CV curves (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0002">2D</a>), indicating that the Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering and P2-O2 phase transition is inhibited after multicomponent modulation.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0028_R_d208945176e847" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Energy Storage Mater">28</a>, <a href="#bte220230022-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0032_R_d208945176e847" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv Energy Mater">32</a></sup></span>&nbsp;In addition, the NNLMTO cathode can also deliver a decent specific capacity of 128 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C, whereas the other two control samples of NNLMO and NNMTO can deliver a specific capacity of 107.9 and 123.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, and their charge/discharge curves are shown in Supporting Information: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>. For the purpose of studying the rate performance, both P2-NNMO and P2-NNLMTO samples are subjected to current densities ranging from 0.1 C to 5 C for testing, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0002">2E,F</a>. It can be intuitively observed that NNLMTO exhibits better rate performance than that of NNMO, in which an excellent specific capacity of 82.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can be achieved at 5 C and recover 96% when the current density back to 0.1 C. The elevated rate performance may be attributed to the enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion rate due to the suppressed Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering and P2-O2 phase transition, which is universally recognized unfavorable to the Na<sup>+</sup> kinetic behaviors,<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0026_R_d208945176e869" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Angew Chem">26</a>, <a href="#bte220230022-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0033_R_d208945176e869" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Sci Adv">33</a>, <a href="#bte220230022-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0047_R_d208945176e869" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Nano Energy">47</a></sup></span> revealing that such a multicomponent modulation strategy can effectively promote Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. The comparison of&nbsp;cyclic stability between NNMO and NNLMTO is also carried out in half cells to investigate the positive effect of structure stability induced by multicomponent modulation, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0002">2G,H</a>. Specifically, the NNLMTO cathode can deliver excellent specific capacity retentions of 85.3% after 50 cycles at 0.1 C and 80% after 200 cycles at 1 C, which is overwhelming compared to the NNMO sample with only 38.2% retention at 0.1 C and 25.2% at 1 C, respectively, revealing the structural stability is enhanced after multicomponent modulation. Moreover, to assess long life-span cyclic stability, the NNLMTO cathode is tested at 5 C (Supporting Information: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>), and such a multicomponent modulation cathode can deliver a gorgeous capacity retention of 83.9% after 500 cycles and 76.3% after 1000 cycles, demonstrating great potential in large-scale energy storage field. In addition, the comparison&nbsp;cyclic stability performance of NNMO, NNLMO, NNMTO, and NNLMTO is also provided in Supporting Information: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, in which the NNLMTO sample exhibits better&nbsp;performance than other samples, indicating that the multicomponent modulation strategy is superior to single Li<sup>+</sup> or Ti<sup>4+</sup> doping. Moreover, the evolution of average operating potential between NNMO and NNLMTO is shown in Supporting Information: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, in which the NNLMTO can deliver an outstanding average operating potential of 3.64 V at 0.1 C with a gorgeous retention of 95.9% after 50 cycles, which is intuitively better than NNMO and superior to most cathode materials up to now.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="bte220230022-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4a12492e-3ba0-4b28-9490-5378fb754827/bte220230022-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4a12492e-3ba0-4b28-9490-5378fb754827/bte220230022-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/01564336-67f7-41b6-8f5e-8028358202ab/bte220230022-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4a12492e-3ba0-4b28-9490-5378fb754827/bte220230022-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=bte220230022-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbte2.20230022" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical properties of half-cells: the first three cycles of charge/discharge curves of (A) Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NNMO) and (B) Na<sub>0.72</sub>Ni<sub>0.28</sub>Li<sub>0.05</sub>Mn<sub>0.57</sub>Ti<sub>0.10</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NNLMTO) at 0.1 C; corresponding cyclic voltammetry curves of (C) NNMO and (D) NNLMTO. (E) The comparison of rate performances between NNMO and NNLMTO with the current density ranging from 0.1 C to 5 C and (F) corresponding charge/discharge curves at different rates. (G, H) Comparison of cyclic performances between NNMO and NNLMTO at the current density of 0.1 C and 1 C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To investigate the structural evolution during the electrochemical measure process, in situ XRD is carried out on the NNLMTO sample during the charge/discharge process. As per our and other previous reports, the P2-O2 phase transition with large volume change occurs in P2-NNMO when charged beyond 4.2 V, leading to severe structure destruction and dramatic&nbsp;capacity decay.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0026_R_d208945176e937" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Angew Chem">26</a>, <a href="#bte220230022-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0054_R_d208945176e937" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv Mater">54</a></sup></span> However, such a P2-O2 phase transition is successfully suppressed in the NNLMTO cathode characterized by the in-situ XRD measurement. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0003">3A,B</a> reveals the evolution process of XRD patterns for NNLMTO during the first charge/discharge process. Upon the charging process, the (002) peak shifts to a lower angle gradually while the (100) peak moves to a higher angle, which reflects an expanded lattice parameter <i>c</i> and the concentration of <i>ab</i> plane during Na<sup>+</sup> deintercalation. This phenomenon can be attributed to the increasing electrostatic repulsive force between TMO<sub>2</sub> layers and the oxidation of transition metal ions when Na<sup>+</sup> content is decreased, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0054_R_d208945176e954" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv Mater">54</a>, <a href="#bte220230022-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0055_R_d208945176e954" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - J Power Sources">55</a></sup></span> In addition, a new peak located at about 16.3° appears when charged beyond 4.2 V, which could be attributed to the emergency of Z phase.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0056_R_d208945176e957" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Energy Environ Sci">56</a>, <a href="#bte220230022-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0057_R_d208945176e957" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Inorganic Chem">57</a></sup></span> Z phase is an intergrowth structure of P-type layers and O-type stacking faults, which is much closer to the P2 structure with fewer stacking faults compared with the O2 phase or OP4 phase.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0054_R_d208945176e960" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv Mater">54</a>, <a href="#bte220230022-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0056_R_d208945176e960" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Energy Environ Sci">56</a></sup></span> Moreover, during the discharge process, all the peaks move back to their original position gradually, indicating the great reversibility of such a P2-Z phase transition. Besides, the detailed lattice parameters evolution during the charge process is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0003">3C</a>, the lattice parameter <i>c</i> declines beyond 4.2 V due to the emergency of the Z phase. However, the calculated volume change during the whole charge process is only 2.87%, which is significantly lower than NNMO with a volume change about 23%, indicating the number O-type stacking faults is much less and contributing to a robust structure. In brief, through in situ XRD analysis of NNLMTO, the destructive P2-O2 phase transition is well suppressed and transformed into a P2-Z phase transition with less stacking faults and better reversibility, which is account for the enhanced structural stability and electrochemical performance after the delicate multicomponent modulation strategy.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="bte220230022-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/eaa7e03a-479e-4a20-a773-7ddd2b9b486a/bte220230022-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/eaa7e03a-479e-4a20-a773-7ddd2b9b486a/bte220230022-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e7bb24d2-0347-4b73-98ae-58a7797e8b6b/bte220230022-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/eaa7e03a-479e-4a20-a773-7ddd2b9b486a/bte220230022-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=bte220230022-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbte2.20230022" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">(A) In situ X-ray diffraction patterns of the Na<sub>0.72</sub>Ni<sub>0.28</sub>Li<sub>0.05</sub>Mn<sub>0.57</sub>Ti<sub>0.10</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NNLMTO) cathode collected during the first charge/discharge process with a current density of 0.2 C. (B) Corresponding intensity contour maps concerning the evolution of the main characteristic peaks of (002) and (100). (C) Corresponding lattice parameters evolution during the charge process.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">To investigate the kinetic behavior related to&nbsp;the multicomponent modulation, galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) measurement is performed on NNLMTO and NNMO samples. The GITT curves of NNLMTO and NNMO are shown in Supporting Information: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8A,B</a>, respectively. It is generally accepted that the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient can be calculated by the followed simplified Equation&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#bte220230022-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a> under condition of the functional relationship between <i>E</i> and <i>τ</i><sup>1/2</sup> is linear<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0058_R_d208945176e1020" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - J Electrochem Soc">58</a>, <a href="#bte220230022-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0059_R_d208945176e1020" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Energy Storage Mater">59</a></sup></span>:

            <div class="inline-equation" id="bte220230022-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/1d7e5811-3fb6-4b05-9088-117cf9ab9b5f/bte220230022-math-0001.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:27681688:media:bte220230022:bte220230022-math-0001" title="urn:x-wiley:27681688:media:bte220230022:bte220230022-math-0001" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>in which <img alt="urn:x-wiley:27681688:media:bte220230022:bte220230022-math-0002" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/3e8b5a17-5fec-4342-bff1-ae91d08bafab/bte220230022-math-0002.png"> (cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>) is the diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> in cathode, <i>V</i><sub>m</sub> and <i>M</i><sub>B</sub> are the molar volume and molar weight of the cathode materials, m<sub><i>B</i></sub> and S are the mass loading of the active materials and the surface area of the electrode (1.13 cm<sup>−2</sup> in this work), respectively.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0060_R_d208945176e1047" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - J Mater Chem A">60</a></sup></span> Supporting Information: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8C</a> demonstrates the parameters of a single step. The <img alt="urn:x-wiley:27681688:media:bte220230022:bte220230022-math-0003" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/d715e890-8f63-4c90-aa02-48443f3528aa/bte220230022-math-0003.png"> in NNLMTO sample can be calculated based on Equation&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#bte220230022-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a> due to the good linear functional relationship between <i>E</i> and <i>τ</i><sup>1/2</sup>, as shown in Supporting Information: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8D</a>. It can be intuitively observed that the <img alt="urn:x-wiley:27681688:media:bte220230022:bte220230022-math-0004" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/7f86b153-5400-4eb8-bc9a-cb17dd71406d/bte220230022-math-0004.png"> in NNLMTO is much higher than that of NNMO in both charge and discharge process (Supporting Information: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8E,F</a>), indicating better Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/deintercalation kinetic behavior of NNLMTO. In addition, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) is also utilized to analysis the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetic behavior, an equivalent circuit is employed to describe&nbsp;the interface, as shown in Supporting Information: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>. The Nyquist plots of NNLMTO and NNMO are shown in Supporting Information: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10A,B</a>, both samples show a semicircle in the high frequency region and an oblique line in the low frequency region, which can be attributed to the charge transfer resistance <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> and Warburg resistance <i>Z</i><sub>w</sub>, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0061_R_d208945176e1090" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - J Power Sources">61</a></sup></span> <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> represents the Na<sup>+</sup> charge transfer resistance on the corresponding interface between the electrode and the electrolyte whereas the <i>Z</i><sub>w</sub> depends on the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> in the bulk of the electrode. It can be observed that NNLMTO possesses a smaller <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> (302.9 Ω) than that of NNMO (556.9 Ω), indicating optimized interface between electrode and electrolyte after multicomponent modulation. Moreover, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient can be calculated through EIS according to the Equation&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#bte220230022-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a><span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0061_R_d208945176e1112" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - J Power Sources">61</a>, <a href="#bte220230022-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0062_R_d208945176e1112" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - ACS Appl Mater Interfaces">62</a></sup></span>:

            <div class="inline-equation" id="bte220230022-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/fad4b6e9-b035-4e0a-bede-8af3b6dcad89/bte220230022-math-0005.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:27681688:media:bte220230022:bte220230022-math-0005" title="urn:x-wiley:27681688:media:bte220230022:bte220230022-math-0005" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>in which <img alt="urn:x-wiley:27681688:media:bte220230022:bte220230022-math-0006" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/f3553954-3bfe-4877-9baf-d05cbee0846d/bte220230022-math-0006.png"> represents the the diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> in cathode, <i>R</i> is the molar gas constant, <i>T</i> is the Kelvin temperature, <i>A</i> is the surface area of the electrode, <i>F</i> is the Faraday's constant, <i>σ</i><sub>ω</sub> is the slope of the functional relationship between <i>Z</i><sub>re</sub> and <i>ω</i><sup>−1/2</sup>, and <i>C</i> is the molar concentration of Na<sup>+</sup> in the cathode material. In addition, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients calculated by Equation&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#bte220230022-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a> for NNLMTO and NNMO (Supporting Information: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10C,D</a>) are 2.02904 × 10<sup>−14</sup> and 0.84841 × 10<sup>−14</sup>, respectively, which also indicates the boosted kinetic behavior after muticomponent modulation, and is consistent with the GITT results.</div>
         
         <p>The charge compensation mechanism of NNLMTO during the charging and discharging process is investigated by ex situ XPS. In the Ni 2<i>p</i> spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0004">4A</a>), the binding energy of Ni shifts to higher energy during the charging process, indicating the oxidation of Ni<sup>2+</sup> into Ni<sup>3+</sup> and Ni<sup>4+</sup>, whereas during the discharging process, the binding energy moves back to lower energy, indicating that the reduction of Ni. It is worthy of&nbsp;note that when discharged to 2.5 V, the Ni element cannot totally recover to the pristine state due to the fact that Ni<sup>3+</sup> cannot be fully reduced to Ni<sup>2+</sup> beyond 2.5 V, which is consistent with the previous report.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0063_R_d208945176e1178" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - Adv Funct Mater">63</a></sup></span> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0004">4B,C</a> exhibit the Mn 2<i>p</i> and Ti 2<i>p</i> spectrum, it can be observed that the binding energy of Mn and Ti are almost unchanged during the charging and discharging process, indicating the electrochemical inertness of Mn and Ti in the NNLMTO sample. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0004">4D</a> shows the O 1<i>s</i> spectrum of NNMLTO, in the pristine state, the peak located at 529.9 eV can be attributed to the lattice oxygen of divalent O<sup>2−</sup>, and the other peaks located at the higher binding energy are ascribed to the oxygenated deposits.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0064_R_d208945176e1196" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - Nano Energy">64</a></sup></span> When charged at 4.35 V, a peak related to the peroxo species (O<sup>−</sup>) is observed with a&nbsp;binding energy of 530.5 eV, indicating the oxidation of O<sup>2−</sup> into O<sup>−</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0052_R_d208945176e1206" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Joule">52</a>, <a href="#bte220230022-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0065_R_d208945176e1206" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - Adv Mater">65</a>, <a href="#bte220230022-bib-0066" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0066_R_d208945176e1206" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 66 - Adv Sci">66</a></sup></span> Furthermore, during the discharge process, the peroxo species peak disappears, indicating that the O element participates in the charge compensation during the charge and discharge process, which is consistent with other works.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0067" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0067_R_d208945176e1209" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 67 - Nano Energy">67</a></sup></span>&nbsp;In addition, the galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of NNLMTO from the first to the fiftieth cycle at the current density of 0.1 C and corresponding d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> curves are presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0004">4E,F</a>. After 50 cycles, the plateau above 4 V in charge/discharge curves and corresponding redox peaks in d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> curves maintains well,<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0068" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0068_R_d208945176e1224" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 68 - Chem Mater">68</a>, <a href="#bte220230022-bib-0069" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0069_R_d208945176e1224" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 69 - Adv Funct Mater">69</a></sup></span> while the plateau and corresponding peaks in the NNMO sample (Supporting Information: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>) almost disappear, indicating the promoted reversibility of lattice oxygen redox reaction after multicomponent modulation.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="bte220230022-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2db77077-e77f-4ab3-8af7-4c65cd71bd95/bte220230022-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/2db77077-e77f-4ab3-8af7-4c65cd71bd95/bte220230022-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c2dc58e0-c8c4-4c81-89c0-1133416135f5/bte220230022-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2db77077-e77f-4ab3-8af7-4c65cd71bd95/bte220230022-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=bte220230022-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbte2.20230022" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Charge compensation mechanism analysis: (A–D) Ex situ X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy analysis of Ni 2<i>p</i>, Mn 2<i>p</i>, Ti 2<i>p</i>, and O 1<i>s</i>, respectively. (E) Galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of Na<sub>0.72</sub>Ni<sub>0.28</sub>Li<sub>0.05</sub>Mn<sub>0.57</sub>Ti<sub>0.10</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NNLMTO) from the first to 50th cycle at the current density of 0.1 C and (F) corresponding d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> curves.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>DFT calculations are employed to provide theoretical support for such a delicate multicomponent modulation strategy. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0005">5A</a> shows the comparison of&nbsp;charge/discharge curves of NNMO and NNLMTO. According to previous reports,<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0033_R_d208945176e1284" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Sci Adv">33</a></sup></span> there are two&nbsp;main major intermediate phases in NNMO, one is Na<sub>0.5</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> at about 3.5 V corresponding to an ordering structure where one row of Na<sub>f</sub> and two rows of Na<sub>e</sub> alternate arrangement in the plane. The other is Na<sub>0.33</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> at about 4 V corresponding to an ordering structure where either Na<sub>f</sub> or Na<sub>e</sub> arrange&nbsp;in rows within a single layer. In addition, the plateaus at 3.3 V and 3.7 V can be concluded attributed to the Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering reactions between NNMO to the intermediate phase Na<sub>0.5</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.5</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> to Na<sub>0.33</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. It can be seen that the two plateaus disappear in the charge/discharge curves of NNLMTO, indicating suppressed Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering reactions can be achieved after such a multicomponent modulation strategy. Moreover, the formation energies of Na sites in NNMO and NNLMTO are also calculated to underlying the mechanism of suppressed Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering reactions, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0005">5B</a>. The energy difference of NNMO is 5.96 eV, whereas in NNLMTO is 2.57 eV, indicating that multicomponent modulation strategy effectively deceases the energy difference between Na<sub><i>e</i></sub> and Na<sub><i>f</i></sub>, which can prohibit the formation of intermediate phase with ordering structure, thus suppressing the Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy reactions and promote solid solution reaction over a wider voltage range during Na<sup>+</sup> de/intercalation.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0031_R_d208945176e1359" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nano Energy">31</a>, <a href="#bte220230022-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0033_R_d208945176e1359" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Sci Adv">33</a></sup></span> The electronic structure is also investigated by DFT calculations in terms of NNMO and NNLMTO, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0005">5C,D</a>. It can be intuitively seen that no density of states can be observed at the Fermi level, indicating a semiconductor feature. However, NNLMTO shows an obvious density of states at the Fermi level for spin-up states and a band gap of 0.426 eV for spin-down states, indicating its half-metallic oxide feature, which is superior in conductivity compared with semiconductor.<span><sup><a href="#bte220230022-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0048_R_d208945176e1366" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Nat Commun">48</a>, <a href="#bte220230022-bib-0070" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220230022-bib-0070_R_d208945176e1366" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 70 - J Power Sources">70</a></sup></span> Thus, the electronic conduction is also enhanced thanks to such a multicomponent modulation strategy.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="bte220230022-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8d6e2221-4cb9-4bd1-9752-4735d082b98f/bte220230022-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8d6e2221-4cb9-4bd1-9752-4735d082b98f/bte220230022-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fd807499-8ab7-496c-9806-7a069dc1ecd9/bte220230022-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8d6e2221-4cb9-4bd1-9752-4735d082b98f/bte220230022-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=bte220230022-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbte2.20230022" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Density functional theory (DFT) calculation: (A) Comparing charging and discharging curves of Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NNMO) and Na<sub>0.72</sub>Ni<sub>0.28</sub>Li<sub>0.05</sub>Mn<sub>0.57</sub>Ti<sub>0.10</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NNLMTO). (B) Calculated formation energy of Na<sub><i>e</i></sub> and Na<sub><i>f</i></sub> in NNMO and NNLMTO. Partial density of states (PDOS) of (C) NNMO and (D) NNLMTO.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>It is universally believed that only the evaluation of electrochemical performance from half cells is not sufficient to demonstrate the practical application potential of the cathode materials. Therefore, the full cell based on the NNLMTO cathode and commercial HC anode (denoted as NNLMTO//HC) is assembled in both coin-type and pouch-type batteries, and the reaction mechanism of charge/discharge process is schematically demonstrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0006">6A</a>. During the charge process, Na<sup>+</sup> extracts from the NNLMTO cathode and inserts into HC anode accompanied by&nbsp;the electron migrating into the anode in the external circuit, and vice versa. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0006">6B,C</a> shows the electrochemical performance at different current densities and the corresponding charge/discharge curves of NNLMTO//HC coin-type full cell, in which the NNLMTO//HC can deliver a decent specific capacity of 122.4, 120.4, 99.0, 83.9, and 70.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (based on the cathode mass) at the discharge rate of 0.1 C, 0.2 C, 0.5 C, 1 C, and 2 C within the voltage range of 2.2–4.35 V, respectively. Moreover, the corresponding Ragone plot calculated based on the total mass of cathode and anode active materials is presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0006">6D</a>, where an outstanding energy density of 263.4 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at the power density of 37.3 W kg<sup>−1</sup> can be achieved. In addition, it can still deliver a decent energy density of 131.2 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> even at an&nbsp;immense power density of 640.3 W kg<sup>−1</sup>. Meanwhile, such a full cell device can deliver an excellent output operating voltage of 3.56 V at the power density of 37.3 W kg<sup>−1</sup> with an insignificant drop when the power density increases (Supporting Information: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>). This well-designed full cell device is competitive when compared with previously reported sodium ion full cells (Supporting Information: Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>). Cycling performance is also a crucial indicator for commercialization, thus the cycling stability evaluation of NNLMTO//HC coin type full cell device is also performed after the rate capability test, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0006">6E,F</a>. Such a coin-type full cell device can deliver a decent capacity retention of 77.5% after 30 cycles at 0.1 C and 78.6% after 100 cycles at 1 C. To further demonstrate the potential in practical application, the NNLMTO//HC full cell is charged into 4.35 V and used to lighten on a circuit board with a “USTC” logo composed of 38 green light-emitting diodes (Supporting Information: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>). What's more, a pouch-type battery with a single layer of NNLMTO cathode and HC anode is assembled to better illustrate the practical application potential, as schematically shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0006">6G</a>. In addition Supporting Information: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> provides the digital image of the real pouch-type full cell with a capacity of 17.7 mAh. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0006">6H</a> shows the galvanostatic charging/discharge curves of such a pouch-type battery, in which a specific capacity of 113.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> based on the cathode mass can be achieved in the voltage range of 1.2–4.35 V. It is worthy to note that the specific capacity of pouch-type full cell is lower than that of the coin-type full cell, this phenomenon may be attributed that the HC electrode in coin-type full cell has been pretreated via a precycling procedure in half cells, which can improve the initial Coulombic efficiency. In contrast, due to the limitation of assembling machines, the HC anode is not pretreated in pouch-type full cell. Moreover, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220230022-fig-0006">6I</a>, such a pouch-type full cell can deliver an excellent capacity retention of 81.2% after 100 cycles at 0.1 C. In general, the good results of the electrochemical evaluation of NNLMTO//HC full cell device illustrate its large potential in practical application in terms of such a multicomponent modulation layered oxide cathode.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="bte220230022-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c4dea237-fb49-49c6-af34-b826500e5fe8/bte220230022-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c4dea237-fb49-49c6-af34-b826500e5fe8/bte220230022-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c2cd0000-c2a0-4c4d-9d6a-27334d779242/bte220230022-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c4dea237-fb49-49c6-af34-b826500e5fe8/bte220230022-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=bte220230022-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbte2.20230022" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Evaluations of the full cell device based on Na<sub>0.72</sub>Ni<sub>0.28</sub>Li<sub>0.05</sub>Mn<sub>0.57</sub>Ti<sub>0.10</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NNLMTO) cathode and hard carbon anode. (A) Schematic diagram of the working principle in full cell configuration. (B) Rate performance and (C) corresponding charge/discharge curves of the coin type full cell. (D) Ragone plot of the relationship between energy density and power density. (E, F) Cycling stability of coin type full cell device at 0.1 C and 1 C rate, respectively. (G) Schematic diagram of NNLMTO//HC pouch type full cell. (H) Charge/discharge curves of pouch-type full cell. (I) Cycling stability of pouch-type full cell.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="bte220230022-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="bte220230022-sec-0030-title">3 CONCLUSION</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we propose a delicate multicomponent modulation strategy to address the P2-O2 phase transition and Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering simultaneously for NNMO. The well-designed NNLMTO cathode material exhibits excellent electrochemical performance with a high capacity of 128 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and an operating voltage of 3.64 V within the voltage of 2.5–4.35 V in half cell configuration. In addition, it also displays a considerable cycling stability of 80% after 200 cycles at 1 C, as well as a brilliant rate performance of 82.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 C. Through in situ XRD measurement, the multicomponent modulation cathode NNLMTO shows a P2-Z phase transition instead of a P2-O2 phase transition, which extremely enhances structure stability. Furthermore, the smooth charge/discharge curves combined with smaller energy differences between Na<sub>e</sub> and Na<sub>f</sub> calculated by DFT indicate better Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy disordering is realized after multicomponent modulation. Moreover, inspired by the gorgeous performance in half cell, the full cell device based on NNLMTO cathode and commercial HC is assembled in both coin-type and pouch-type, in which the coin-type full cell can deliver an excellent energy density of 263.4 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at 37.3 W kg<sup>−1</sup>, whereas a pouch-type full cell exhibits a fabulous capacity retention of 81.2% after 100 cycles at 0.1 C, demonstrating significant practicality. Our work demonstrates the positive effect of multicomponent modulation in sodium-based layered oxide design and provides insights to develop high-performance cathodes for sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="bte220230022-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="bte220230022-sec-0040-title">4 EXPERIMENTAL SECTION</h2>
         
         <p>Experimental details are provided in the Supporting Information.</p>
         </section>
